  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4ec87e1f-a8c8-46d3-aeb8-21a6ed5430d3'

              <span id="reviewTextContainer424851161" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9240915829246310987">Naïve. Super, Erlend Loe<br/>Naïve. Super. is a novel by the Norwegian Erlend Loe. It was first published in 1996 in Norwegian, where it was very popular. The story is narrated by a man in his mid-twenties who suddenly becomes disillusioned and confused by life and therefore quits university. The narrator becomes fascinated by both modern scientific theories of time and relativity. He reads a book by Paul Davies and also engages in repetitive childish activities such as playing with wooden BRIO child</span>
  <span id="freeText9240915829246310987" style="display:none">Naïve. Super, Erlend Loe<br />Naïve. Super. is a novel by the Norwegian Erlend Loe. It was first published in 1996 in Norwegian, where it was very popular. The story is narrated by a man in his mid-twenties who suddenly becomes disillusioned and confused by life and therefore quits university. The narrator becomes fascinated by both modern scientific theories of time and relativity. He reads a book by Paul Davies and also engages in repetitive childish activities such as playing with wooden BRIO children's toys and repeatedly throwing a ball against a wall. In the end, the narrator visits his brother in New York City and returns to Norway with a renewed sense of meaning in life.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بیست و هفتم ماه آوریل سال 2014 م<br />عنوان: اَبَر ابله ؛ نویسنده: ارلند لو؛ مترجم: شقایق قندهاری؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، ثالث، 1389، در 231 ص، شابک: 9789643806347؛ چاپ سوم 1393؛ چاپ چهارم 1395؛ چاپ پنجم 1397؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان نروژی - سده 20 م<br /><br />روزنگاشته های پسری بیست و پنج ساله است، شادی درون در خود نهفته دارد. بار نخست که بخوانید ابلهانه و ساده است اما دیگر بار که بخوانید روانشناسی آدمهاست. اگر زمان با شما یار بود، بار سوم هم بخوانید و مدارا کنید. داستان با این جمله ها آغاز میشود: «من دو دوست دارم، یک دوست خوب و یک دوست بد. و البته برادرم هم هست...»؛ پایان نقل. ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15837841492727528233">A charming story of a different kind of person! A person who still has a deeply rooted connection to childhood at 25, who refuses to do things that seem meaningless, who carefully thinks twice before making simple choices, but who dares to admit feeling scared when thinking of the "big questions" in life.<br/> <br/>Undoubtedly, the narrator seems a bit odd, but I closed the book with a smile on my face and the suspicion that it actually is much more strange to go on repeating regular patterns infinitely w</span>
  <span id="freeText15837841492727528233" style="display:none">A charming story of a different kind of person! A person who still has a deeply rooted connection to childhood at 25, who refuses to do things that seem meaningless, who carefully thinks twice before making simple choices, but who dares to admit feeling scared when thinking of the "big questions" in life.<br /> <br />Undoubtedly, the narrator seems a bit odd, but I closed the book with a smile on my face and the suspicion that it actually is much more strange to go on repeating regular patterns infinitely without understanding them than to break out and find patterns that make at least temporary sense: like throwing a ball against a wall or making lists of good and important things.<br /><br />Money is not important. Brothers are. <br /><br />The simple conclusion makes sense, and I hope the narrator will find good use for his newfound "perspective". It changed mine to read his tale.</span>

<div id="review_2474517574" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2474517574" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2474517574?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2474517574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13723518641192304240">Back in 2006 or so Amazon had this “Listmania” feature where, youse guessed it, people made lists of stuff to share with others. I’d waste way too much time browsing those lists but I’d get recs for some pretty decent books to read that were similar to books I’d read before. One such book that appeared a lot was Erlend Loe’s Naive. Super, which I read a few pages of and decided wasn’t for me. Cut to the other week, some 10+ years after that encounter, when I happened to see it on a bookshelf and</span>
  <span id="freeText13723518641192304240" style="display:none">Back in 2006 or so Amazon had this “Listmania” feature where, youse guessed it, people made lists of stuff to share with others. I’d waste way too much time browsing those lists but I’d get recs for some pretty decent books to read that were similar to books I’d read before. One such book that appeared a lot was Erlend Loe’s Naive. Super, which I read a few pages of and decided wasn’t for me. Cut to the other week, some 10+ years after that encounter, when I happened to see it on a bookshelf and decided, what the hey, I’ll finally read it cover to cover. Mebbe I was wrong? Nope, my instincts was right the first time because this novel suuuuucked! <br /><br />A boring Norwegian twentysomething, who’s probably on the autism spectrum, had a meltdown and dropped out of college. Now he hangs around an apartment bouncing a ball, repeatedly hammering pegs and making inane lists (hey, like Amazon’s Listmania!). About halfway through his brother gets him to fly out to visit him in America and he makes lists in America. The end. <br /><br />Whaaat? I know it wasn’t entertaining so was this pigslop meant to be deep and profound? Because it weren’t! The guy is also obsessed with this scientist who writes about space and bangs on about space trivia, so maybe it was trying for a pseudo-philosophical angle, but it completely failed. <br /><br />And then there’s the pages of rubbish like the printouts of mid-90s library catalogue searches with Norwegian swears - was that meant to be funny? Or just to highlight the main character’s probable autism by showing how he scrapbooks his life in an OCD way like the main character of Mark Haddon’s The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time? It wasn’t funny, it seemed pointless - it was just another aspect of the book that went way over my head or had no meaning anyway. And you can tell it’s the ‘90s too as everyone faxes each other instead of texting/emailing. Nothing worthwhile either so humans have remained boring idiots whatever technology we’re playing with! <br /><br />Nowt happens in Naive. Super and it’s a total crapwad novel. I’d say this was a case of style over substance but - what style? </span>
  <a data-text-id="13723518641192304240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_159256805" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159256805" />
    <a title="Anita Dalton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3205057-anita-dalton"><img alt="Anita Dalton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540437688p2/3205057.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/159256805?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anita Dalton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anita Dalton" href="/user/show/3205057-anita-dalton">Anita Dalton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3205057-anita-dalton?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3205057-anita-dalton?shelf=irob-review-2011">irob-review-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3205057-anita-dalton?shelf=gently-weird">gently-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3205057-anita-dalton?shelf=books-we-own">books-we-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159256805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4375427512438675759">Of course, this is the sort of novel that could only happen in a relatively benign place like Norway where a grown man playing with a little boy doesn’t inspire the need in every passerby to call Chris Hansen, and to a person who has a brother with an empty apartment. The protagonist is the most earnest character an American like me can possibly hope to read. With so many novels so sickeningly drenched in irony, the protagonist in Naïve. Super is completely devoid of it. And because he is not se</span>
  <span id="freeText4375427512438675759" style="display:none">Of course, this is the sort of novel that could only happen in a relatively benign place like Norway where a grown man playing with a little boy doesn’t inspire the need in every passerby to call Chris Hansen, and to a person who has a brother with an empty apartment. The protagonist is the most earnest character an American like me can possibly hope to read. With so many novels so sickeningly drenched in irony, the protagonist in Naïve. Super is completely devoid of it. And because he is not self-referentially hip in his depression and his attempts to make meaning of his world, it is tempting to write him off as simple, possibly stupid. It means something when, confronted with earnestness and a complete lack of irony, it is tempting to dismiss it as lacking intellect. I’m too tired to discuss what that means, but believe me when I say the protagonist is not mentally retarded or otherwise lacking in intellect. He’s just finding himself in a manner that does not involve utter self-destruction and the delivery of oh-so-clever one-liners. That having been said, in the midst of such simplicity, this is a deeply funny book.<br /><br />The protagonist begins the novel explaining that he has two friends, one good and one bad, and his brother, who is less friendly than him, but a good guy nonetheless. One has to agree that his brother is a good man, because he permits his 25-year-old brother, a man who rather enjoys spending hours playing with Brio toys and making seemingly pointless lists, stay in his apartment in exchange for just giving him his messages. When his brother returns from his trip, he realizes the protagonist is having a gentle nervous breakdown combined with a mild existential crisis, and cares for the protagonist, including taking him on a trip to New York. Perhaps the brother senses that brutally beating the protagonist at croquet was what initially made the protagonist feel like there was no purpose to life. Even so, it’s hard to fault the brother. People who need a villain in a book will not like Naïve. Super because this is a novel filled with nice people. Nice, quirky and not entirely familiar people, but nice people nonetheless. <a href="http://ireadoddbooks.com/naive-super-by-erlend-loe/" rel="nofollow noopener">You can read my entire review here.</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="4375427512438675759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_364481080" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/364481080" />
    <a title="Matthew Rhodes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10809742-matthew-rhodes"><img alt="Matthew Rhodes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1359068062p2/10809742.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/364481080?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Rhodes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Rhodes" href="/user/show/10809742-matthew-rhodes">Matthew Rhodes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer364481080" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12586455781011034444">Naive Super is probably the most influential book I've read in terms of my own writing.<br/><br/>This is a brave book. Dialogue is just discarded for the most part. The unnamed narrator is almost anti-protagonist. He is a nice guy. There aren't many books purely about nice guys. There are, however, countless good works about nice guys battling against adversity. The interesting thing about Naive Super is that the adversity is the character's own doing. The adversity is his depression. Almost like a quarte</span>
  <span id="freeText12586455781011034444" style="display:none">Naive Super is probably the most influential book I've read in terms of my own writing.<br /><br />This is a brave book. Dialogue is just discarded for the most part. The unnamed narrator is almost anti-protagonist. He is a nice guy. There aren't many books purely about nice guys. There are, however, countless good works about nice guys battling against adversity. The interesting thing about Naive Super is that the adversity is the character's own doing. The adversity is his depression. Almost like a quarter-life crisis.<br /><br />I suppose this is a short book about the feeling of not knowing what to do any more. The worrying feeling that barely anything is happening in your life. Ultimately, what is the point? What is important? What is going wrong?<br /><br />What makes this book so good is the dry humour of the narrator. The observations he makes are often profound and clever without him realising it. The narration is also deceptively simple. You will find no detailed descriptions in this book. The sentences are always short and direct.<br /><br />In this sense, I would not recommend this for everybody. Not an awful lot happens in this book, but the points made by the narrator are often sublime and at times quite heartening, when you know you have been/are in the same place as this character.<br /><br />In terms of my own writing, I found this book really intriguing. It's almost like a minimal novel which appeals to me. There are not many characters. The main character is flawed but he is fully aware of that. The drama is in the everyday life, not from landmark moments. The narrator is not a unique character. I feel that he is someone that a lot of readers can relate to and very easily put themselves in his exact same position.<br /><br />In short: a funny, clever, moving and ambitious short novel that you could easily read in the space of two days. Excellent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12586455781011034444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_265056127" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265056127" />
    <a title="Sussu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7333612-sussu"><img alt="Sussu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603714525p2/7333612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sussu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sussu" href="/user/show/7333612-sussu">Sussu</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9789515789631" href="/book/show/3177709-supernaiivi">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265056127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11016659989691969078">I've read this book many a time before, and it has yet to fail me. It's a simple book on the surface, but for me it's a way to climb out of a hole whenever existing gets to be a bit too much. It's not a magic cure, but it makes life tolerable again.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1381179537" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16739725598811370019"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/129068048393/na%C3%AFve-super-by-erlend-loe'>http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/1290680...</a><br/><br/>A refreshing look at a young man from Norway trying to make sense of his life and attempting to find meaning in it. Perhaps the narrator is a bit too serious or obsessive in his assessments over the purpose of his life. He thinks constantly about the question of time and space. His over-adjustment to this uncomfortable predicament is to simply throw a ball against a wall all night or repeatedly hammer wooden pegs into a board. And this might seem too juven</span>
  <span id="freeText16739725598811370019" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/129068048393/na%C3%AFve-super-by-erlend-loe">http://msarki.tumblr.com/post/1290680...</a><br /><br />A refreshing look at a young man from Norway trying to make sense of his life and attempting to find meaning in it. Perhaps the narrator is a bit too serious or obsessive in his assessments over the purpose of his life. He thinks constantly about the question of time and space. His over-adjustment to this uncomfortable predicament is to simply throw a ball against a wall all night or repeatedly hammer wooden pegs into a board. And this might seem too juvenile based on the literary and scientific interests of the narrator. Of course he isn’t dumb, only childish in his new behavior. But finding a girlfriend helps, as does a trip overseas to NYC to visit his brother. Having fun and playing games instead of worrying all the time becomes his new reason for living, and in this process he learns to love and be loved. But the question remains throughout the entire book if it will be okay for him in the end. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1973853759" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1973853759" />
    <a title="Apeksha Shetty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28389851-apeksha-shetty"><img alt="Apeksha Shetty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516511558p2/28389851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1973853759?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apeksha Shetty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apeksha Shetty" href="/user/show/28389851-apeksha-shetty">Apeksha Shetty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1973853759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11181274079240867524">If someone could have every uncensored thought in their head over a few days typed out and rolled into a book, this book would be it. It speaks to you like a best friend would. With no intention of seeming cool, and every intention of being true.<br/><br/><i>‘My brother nods. He asks me whether I have ever considered thinking less.<br/>I tell him I consider it all the time, but that it’s not that easy.’</i><br/><br/>The unnamed lead protagonist is a nice guy. And he’s just that, a nice guy. No frills attached. But he’s a nice </span>
  <span id="freeText11181274079240867524" style="display:none">If someone could have every uncensored thought in their head over a few days typed out and rolled into a book, this book would be it. It speaks to you like a best friend would. With no intention of seeming cool, and every intention of being true.<br /><br /><i>‘My brother nods. He asks me whether I have ever considered thinking less.<br />I tell him I consider it all the time, but that it’s not that easy.’</i><br /><br />The unnamed lead protagonist is a nice guy. And he’s just that, a nice guy. No frills attached. But he’s a nice guy faced with a bout of existential crisis, as I would like to believe most of us are.<br />The book flows through his thoughts and attempts to find out if “everything will be alright in the end.”<br /><br />He is brutally honest, the kind of honest one can only be when they are at their lowest. A strange thing happens when you’re extremely low. Your search for respite trumps your need for societal validation, and what entails is a truly glorious trail of unfiltered (albeit muddled) thought. <br /><br />There are no fancy words. It is devoid of this recent obsession with irony that I’m just.so.sick.of. It just has a bunch of extremely endearing characters, particularly the lead, who you can’t help but like. And lots of periods.<br /><br />As it so happens, there isn’t much about Norway itself in the book, which was a bit of a downer. But I feel like the treatment of feelings, the utter disregard for facades and simplistic outlook could only be a product of the lack of urban complications.<br /><br />That being said, this book probably won’t appeal to everyone. I don’t know what it takes for someone to be able to grasp the profundity beneath its deceptively simple prose. Maybe it takes having been where the character is. Maybe it takes an eager need for simplicity. Or maybe the need to not have to read a sentence over and over to grasp its brilliance. Maybe it isn’t profound at all and I have concocted an entirely fantastical meaning from the book, and Erlend might be confounded by what I have made of it. But who cares. I’m going to recommend this book to ever one who feels a little stuck. Or doesn’t. Chances are you could do with a little detangling.<br /><br /><i>‘I still don’t know if things fit together, or if everything will be all right in the end. But I believe that something means something. I believe in cleansing the soul through fun and games. I also believe in love.’</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11181274079240867524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4027908301165394946">This is my second novel from Loe and while it was pretty good I didn't like it as much as Lazy days. <br/><br/>The book examins the same topics as Lazy days; finding meaning. The theme was different though, instead of looking at it in the context of a middle aged couple it shifted focus to a young man in his twenties. <br/><br/>Our protagonist is a caricature of what we often seen in the 90's generation.   <span id="freeText4027908301165394946" style="display:none">This is my second novel from Loe and while it was pretty good I didn't like it as much as Lazy days. <br /><br />The book examins the same topics as Lazy days; finding meaning. The theme was different though, instead of looking at it in the context of a middle aged couple it shifted focus to a young man in his twenties. <br /><br />Our protagonist is a caricature of what we often seen in the 90's generation. Desperately looking for something to do which actually has a meaning yet unable to find anything that truly interests him and has potential. There is an underlying existential anxiety which has a paralyzing effect. The activities he starts in the end only have a self serving purpose, that is to reduce the anxiety, but have no meaning in itself so they further increase it in the end. <br /><br />A great picture but for me the ending wasn't strong enough. It seemed like the point where reality really turned into a fairy tale. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4027908301165394946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer305101284480721610">The title doesn't lie; our narrator's questions do at times read <i>(super?)</i> naive, as they examine the very basics of self, ponder the fundamentals of modern existence, observe commercialism, and catalog varied random minutia. <br/><br/>This whole thing felt rather like a mash-up of other, previously familiar ingredients: the questioning of capitalism, modern society and one's role in it from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2546.Chuck_Palahniuk' title='Chuck Palahniuk' rel='nofollow noopener'>Chuck Palahniuk</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36236124.Fight_Club' title='Fight Club by Chuck Palahniuk' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fight Club</a> <i>(sans the mayhem and violence)</i>, the questioning of universe, and the difficulties in co</span>
  <span id="freeText305101284480721610" style="display:none">The title doesn't lie; our narrator's questions do at times read <i>(super?)</i> naive, as they examine the very basics of self, ponder the fundamentals of modern existence, observe commercialism, and catalog varied random minutia. <br /><br />This whole thing felt rather like a mash-up of other, previously familiar ingredients: the questioning of capitalism, modern society and one's role in it from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2546.Chuck_Palahniuk" title="Chuck Palahniuk" rel="nofollow noopener">Chuck Palahniuk</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36236124.Fight_Club" title="Fight Club by Chuck Palahniuk" rel="nofollow noopener">Fight Club</a> <i>(sans the mayhem and violence)</i>, the questioning of universe, and the difficulties in connecting with humanity <i>(both with one's own and with others)</i> from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/76360.Matt_Haig" title="Matt Haig" rel="nofollow noopener">Matt Haig</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16130537.The_Humans" title="The Humans by Matt Haig" rel="nofollow noopener">The Humans</a>, as well as the whimsy, the childlike innocence, and the childhood ignorance for societal expectations present in works of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/52277.Adam_Rapp" title="Adam Rapp" rel="nofollow noopener">Adam Rapp</a> <i>(sans the grit)</i>. <br /><br />A pleasantly mild, self-searching cocktail; frequently amusing, occasionally touching. Sweet and easy read.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2730280674" />
    <a title="Aino" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58077210-aino"><img alt="Aino" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469726834p2/58077210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer4829998428123487049">I love this little book!<br/>At first it felt like such a cliché—young man drops out of uni due to existential crisis caused by too much intellectualising. But then he bought a ball and I liked that. And I liked the lists and the simple writing and how earnest it is. For some rea</span>
  <span id="freeText4829998428123487049" style="display:none">I love this little book!<br />At first it felt like such a cliché—young man drops out of uni due to existential crisis caused by too much intellectualising. But then he bought a ball and I liked that. And I liked the lists and the simple writing and how earnest it is. Clichés are so relatable! <br />He focuses on time a lot and I wonder if the author has read Heidegger and if he has I wonder if he understood it and if he did I wonder if the idea of living an "authentic" life resonated with him. For some reason this book reminds me of that concept. Which I definitely don't really understand. <br /><br />I'm proud and glad that I read this book in one busy day.<br /><br />"I hope I'll be able to say the same if someone asks me to summarise my life in sixty years. That I can just mull it over for a while and say that I think life can be compared to a journey. And feel that I'm having that thought for the first time. That I came up with it myself and that I mean it. <br />So far, I'm absolutely unable to say anything like that. There are too many confusing elements present. Things I know. Thoughts I have. Sarcasm. Things I think I ought to be doing and places I ought to be going. Always other places."<br /><br />"I believe in cleansing the soul through fun and games. I also believe in love."<br /><br />"Now I'm drinking a sip of water, hoping that life will last." <br /></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2754137075" />
    <a title="William Miles" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1551046-william-miles"><img alt="William Miles" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222051086p2/1551046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2754137075?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2754137075" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11749775134931318494">I read this because of the scuttlebutt among appreciators of good books that Pete Buttigieg learned Swedish just to read more books by Norwegian author Erlend Loe, and I wanted to understand what Pete's thinking might have been. A Norwegian friend who I consulted also recommended the author. And this happens to be the only book (on Amazon) by Erlend Loe to be translated into English.<br/><br/>The premise is deceptively simple and quirky, and I loved almost every page and every inventive and odd turn! The </span>
  <span id="freeText11749775134931318494" style="display:none">I read this because of the scuttlebutt among appreciators of good books that Pete Buttigieg learned Swedish just to read more books by Norwegian author Erlend Loe, and I wanted to understand what Pete's thinking might have been. A Norwegian friend who I consulted also recommended the author. And this happens to be the only book (on Amazon) by Erlend Loe to be translated into English.<br /><br />The premise is deceptively simple and quirky, and I loved almost every page and every inventive and odd turn! The book takes place in 1996. A young man reaches his 25 year milestone, and has so many (too many!) questions about life. He leaves his graduate school program to think and be. He even ends up taking a week-long trip to New York City with his brother. The young man is very uncertain about leaving Noway for this trip (now that he has established a little post-academic routine (including a possible romance), but his brother insists that the journey will give him "perspective." <br /><br />Great book, overall. But I have so much else to read that I don't know if I will learn Norwegian to read more of Loe's writing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11749775134931318494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5144822113175706133">3.5 stars.<br/>This book was....charming, yes that is the exact word for this novel. The language used was so simple, but at the same time the author conveyed so easily everyhting he needed. And the way it was written reminded me a bit the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36576608-flowers-for-algernon' rel='nofollow noopener'>Flowers for Algernon</a>. I liked the book overall and it made me to think about the things that excited me in my childhood. I'll post the list of things later.<br/></span>
  <span id="freeText5144822113175706133" style="display:none">3.5 stars.<br />This book was....charming, yes that is the exact word for this novel. <script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UPMq5fiIFqQzG3ll0m0MPSoI5IIjAaU541OYQiakrBLXD89Ym3nDvd5+UkP9MXrgyVlbsj9w2oljcW7wQTrnFw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9cplM76XqGsYfDwtPR0PsiCcelD8EuepbUZTm3Tkj5lyNoCO3WZ9cvUZFwsSQXlvw83FYOBjmBntZKUpE3rEnA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

He asked them to take out their school edition of The Wild Duck. He was once more struck by their hostile attitude toward him. But it couldn\'t be helped, he had a task to perform and was going through with it. It was from them as a group that he sensed that massive dislike sent forth by their bodies. Individually they could be very pleasant, but together, positioned like now, at their desks, they constituted a structural enmity, directed at him and all that he stood for.\n  \n\n  \nElias Rukla begins yet another day under the leaden Oslo sky. At the high school where he teaches, a novel insight into Ibsen\'s The Wild Duck grips him with a passion so intense that he barely notices the disinterest of his students. After the lesson, when a broken umbrella provokes an unpredictable rage, he barely notices the students\' intense curiosity. He soon realizes, however, that this day will be the decisive day of his life.\n\nWith Shyness and Dignity, Dag Solstad - praised in Norway as one of the most innovative novelists of his generation - Foreldrene er ikke der lenger. Broren Luka er syk og trenger akutt hjelp. Mariam bestemmer seg for å flykte ut av Syria, til Tyrkia, og videre med en drøm om å komme til verdens beste land. Dette er en fortelling om ei ung jente som slåss for å redde livet. Historien er realistisk og hjerteskjærende fra et krigsherjet land, hvor konflikten synes uløselig. Midt oppi det hele flykter mange barn og unge helt alene, i håp om å få et bedre liv. Fortelling for ungdomstrinnet.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/211631.Erlend_Loe">About Erlend Loe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13829997285970655624">Erlend Loe is a Norwegian novelist. He worked at a psychiatric clinic, and was later a freelance journalist for Norwegian newspaper Adresseavisen. Loe now lives and works in Oslo where in 1998 he co-founded Screenwriters Oslo - an office community for screenwriters.<br/><br/>In 1993 he debuted with the book Tatt av kvinnen, and a year later published a children's book, Fisken, about a forklift operator name</span>
  <span id="freeText13829997285970655624" style="display:none">Erlend Loe is a Norwegian novelist. He worked at a psychiatric clinic, and was later a freelance journalist for Norwegian newspaper Adresseavisen. Loe now lives and works in Oslo where in 1998 he co-founded Screenwriters Oslo - an office community for screenwriters.<br /><br />In 1993 he debuted with the book Tatt av kvinnen, and a year later published a children's book, Fisken, about a forklift operator named Kurt. Loe has a distinctive style of writing which is often likened to naïve art. He often uses irony, exaggeration and humor. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13829997285970655624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/950536-naiv-super">Quotes from Naïve. Super</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;A human being weighing 70 kilograms contains among other things:<br />-45 litres of water<br />-Enough chalk to whiten a chicken pen<br />-Enough phosphorus for 2,200 matches<br />-Enough fat to make approximately 70 bars of soap<br />-Enough iron to make a two inch nail<br />-Enough carbon for 9,000 pencil points<br />-A spoonful of magnesium<br />I weigh more than 70 kilograms.<br /><br />And I remember a TV series called Cosmos. Carl Sagan would walk around on a set that was meant to look like space, speaking in large numbers. On one of the shows he sat in front of a tank full of all the substances human beings are made of. He stirred the tank with a stick wondering if he would be able to create life.<br />He didn’t succeed.<br />&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/129588-a-human-being-weighing-70-kilograms-contains-among-other-things">64 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;We shall never meet, but there is something I want you to know. My time is not the same as your time. Our times are not the same. And do you know what that means? That means that time does not exist. Do you want me to repeat that? There is no time. There is a life and a death. There are people and animals. Our thoughts exist. And the world. The universe, too. But there is no time. You might as well take it easy. Do you feel better now? I feel better. This is going to work out. Have a nice day.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/660664-we-shall-never-meet-but-there-is-something-i-want">61 likes</a>
            </nobr>
